mw-list-item"><a href="https://tr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikrogliya" title="Mikrogliya – Turkish" lang="tr" hreflang="tr" data-title="Mikrogliya" data-language-autonym="Türkçe" data-language-local-name="Turkish" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Türkçe</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-uk mw-list-item"><a href="https://uk.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9C%D1%96%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BB%D1%96%D1%8F" title="Мікроглія – Ukrainian" lang="uk" hreflang="uk" data-title="Мікроглія" data-language-autonym="Українська" data-language-local-name="Ukrainian" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Українська</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-uz mw-list-item"><a href="https://uz.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikrogliya" title="Mikrogliya – Uzbek" lang="uz" hreflang="uz" data-title="Mikrogliya" data-language-autonym="Oʻzbekcha / ўзбекча" data-language-local-name="Uzbek" 			</ul>
			<div class="after-portlet after-portlet-lang"><span class="wb-langlinks-edit wb-langlinks-link"><a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Special:EntityPage/Q1622829#sitelinks-wikipedia" title="Edit interlanguage links" class="wbc-editpage">Edit links</a></span></div>

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>

							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Glial cell located throughout the brain and spinal cord</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1277584984">@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox_microanatomy tr:nth-of-type(-n/**/+4) [src$=PNG],html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox_microanatomy tr:nth-of-type(-n/**/+4) [src$=png]{background:white}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox_microanatomy tr:nth-of-type(-n/**/+4) [src$=PNG],html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox_microanatomy tr:nth-of-type(-n/**/+4) [src$=png]{background:white}}</style>
<div class="infobox&#95;microanatomy"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316064257">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent;color:inherit}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:-3px}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media(min-width:640px){body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table{display:table!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>caption{display:table-caption!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>tbody{display:table-row-group}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table th,body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table td{padding-left:inherit;padding-right:inherit}}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="background-color:dimgray; color: white">Microglia</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Mikroglej_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Mikroglej_1.jpg/330px-Mikroglej_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="216" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Mikroglej_1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="357" /></a></span><div class="infobox-caption">Microglia in resting state from rat cortex before traumatic brain injury (lectin staining with <a href="/wiki/Horseradish_peroxidase" title="Horseradish peroxidase">HRP</a>)</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Makrofagi_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Makrofagi_2.jpg/330px-Makrofagi_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="244" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Makrofagi_2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="499" data-file-height="419" /></a></span><div class="infobox-caption">Microglia/macrophage – activated form from rat cortex after traumatic brain injury (lectin staining with HRP)</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #efefef;color:inherit;">Details</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">Precursor</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Primitive yolk-sac derived macrophage</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Organ_system" title="Organ system">System</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Central nervous system</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #efefef;color:inherit;">Identifiers</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Medical_Subject_Headings" title="Medical Subject Headings">MeSH</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://meshb.nlm.nih.gov/record/ui?ui=D017628">D017628</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Terminologia_Histologica" title="Terminologia Histologica">TH</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ifaa.unifr.ch/Public/EntryPage/PDF/TH%20Chapter%20H2.00.pdf">H2.00.06.2.00004, H2.00.06.2.01025 </a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Foundational_Model_of_Anatomy" title="Foundational Model of Anatomy">FMA</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://bioportal.bioontology.org/ontologies/FMA/?p=classes&amp;conceptid=http%3A%2F%2Fpurl.org%2Fsig%2Font%2Ffma%2Ffma54539">54539</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below"><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_microanatomy" title="Anatomical terms of microanatomy">Anatomical terms of microanatomy</a><div style="text-align: right;"><small class="noprint">&#91;<a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q1622829" class="extiw" title="d:Q1622829">edit on Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p><b>Microglia</b> are a type of <a href="/wiki/Glia" title="Glia">glial cell</a> located throughout the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia account for about around 5–10% of cells found within the brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lana&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lana-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As the resident <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophage</a> cells, they act as the first and main form of active immune defense in the CNS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Filiano&#95;2015&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Filiano_2015-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia originate in the yolk sac under tightly regulated molecular conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dermitzakis&#95;2023&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dermitzakis_2023-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These cells (and other neuroglia including <a href="/wiki/Astrocyte" title="Astrocyte">astrocytes</a>) are distributed in large non-overlapping regions throughout the CNS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kreutzberg&#95;1995&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kreutzberg_1995-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bushong&#95;2002&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bushong_2002-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia are key cells in overall brain maintenance&#160;&#8211;&#32;they are constantly scavenging the CNS for <a href="/wiki/Amyloid_plaques" title="Amyloid plaques">plaques</a>, damaged or unnecessary <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>, and infectious agents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since these processes must be efficient to prevent potentially fatal damage, microglia are extremely sensitive to even small pathological changes in the CNS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dissing-Olesen&#95;2007&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dissing-Olesen_2007-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This sensitivity is achieved in part by the presence of unique <a href="/wiki/Potassium_channel" title="Potassium channel">potassium channels</a> that respond to even small changes in <a href="/wiki/Extracellular" class="mw-redirect" title="Extracellular">extracellular</a> potassium.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent evidence shows that microglia are also key players in the sustainment of normal brain functions under healthy conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia also constantly monitor neuronal functions through direct somatic contacts via their <b>microglial processes</b>, and exert neuroprotective effects when needed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-epub.ub.uni-muenchen.de&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epub.ub.uni-muenchen.de-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nimmerjahn&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmerjahn-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The brain and spinal cord, which make up the CNS, are not usually accessed directly by pathogenic factors in the body's circulation due to a series of <a href="/wiki/Endothelial_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Endothelial cell">endothelial cells</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>, or BBB. The BBB prevents most infections from reaching the vulnerable nervous tissue. In the case where infectious agents are directly introduced to the brain or cross the blood–brain barrier, microglial cells must react quickly to decrease <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> and destroy the infectious agents before they damage the sensitive neural tissue. Due to the lack of <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a> from the rest of the body (few antibodies are small enough to cross the blood–brain barrier), microglia must be able to recognize foreign bodies, swallow them, and act as <a href="/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell" title="Antigen-presenting cell">antigen-presenting cells</a> activating <a href="/wiki/T-cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cell">T-cells</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The ability to view and characterize different neural cells including microglia began in 1880 when Nissl staining was developed by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Nissl" title="Franz Nissl">Franz Nissl</a>. Franz Nissl and William Ford Robertson first described microglial cells during their <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a> experiments. The cell staining techniques in the 1880s showed that microglia are related to <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>. The activation of microglia and formation of ramified microglial clusters was first noted by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Babe%C8%99" title="Victor Babeș">Victor Babeș</a> while studying a <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> case in 1897. Babeș noted the cells were found in a variety of <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viral</a> brain infections but did not know what the clusters of microglia he saw were.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Babes&#95;1892&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Babes_1892-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Spanish scientist <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a> defined a "third element" (cell type) besides neurons and astrocytes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/P%C3%ADo_del_R%C3%ADo_Hortega" title="Pío del Río Hortega">Pío del Río Hortega</a>, a student of <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a>, first called the cells "microglia" around 1920. He went on to characterize microglial response to brain lesions in 1927 and note the "fountains of microglia" present in the corpus callosum and other perinatal <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a> areas in 1932. After many years of research Rio Hortega became generally considered as the "father of microglia".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rio-Hortega&#95;1892&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rio-Hortega_1892-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rio-Hortega&#95;1937&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rio-Hortega_1937-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For a long period of time little improvement was made in our knowledge of microglia. Then, in 1988, Hickey and Kimura showed that perivascular microglial cells are bone-marrow derived, and express high levels of <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_II" title="MHC class II">MHC class II</a> proteins used for antigen presentation. This confirmed Pio Del Rio-Hortega's postulate that microglial cells functioned similarly to <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> by performing <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presentation" title="Antigen presentation">antigen presentation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>At the end of the 20th century, the experimental psychology group at Oxford University classified microglial cells into 3 types according to their morphology, tissue location and duration of phagocytic activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, many researchers around the world are trying to establish a relationship between microglial cell morphology and the levels of expression of immune mediators by microglial cells, using different software.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Forms">Forms</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Forms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Microglia_and_neurons.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Microglia_and_neurons.jpg/250px-Microglia_and_neurons.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Microglia_and_neurons.jpg/500px-Microglia_and_neurons.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Rat microglia grown in tissue culture in green, along with nerve fiber processes shown in red.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Microglial_cells_(red)_in_rat_cerebellar_molecular_layer.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Microglial_cells_%28red%29_in_rat_cerebellar_molecular_layer.jpg/250px-Microglial_cells_%28red%29_in_rat_cerebellar_molecular_layer.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Microglial_cells_%28red%29_in_rat_cerebellar_molecular_layer.jpg/500px-Microglial_cells_%28red%29_in_rat_cerebellar_molecular_layer.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="919" data-file-height="919" /></a><figcaption>Microglia in rat cerebellar molecular layer in red, stained with antibody to IBA1/AIF1. Bergmann glia processes are shown in green, DNA in blue.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Microglial cells are extremely <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">plastic</a>, and undergo a variety of structural changes based on location and system needs. This level of plasticity is required to fulfill the vast variety of functions that microglia perform. The ability to transform distinguishes microglia from <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>, which must be replaced on a regular basis, and provides them the ability to defend the CNS on extremely short notice without causing immunological disturbance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia adopt a specific form, or <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>, in response to the local conditions and chemical signals they have detected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Verkhratsky&#95;2013&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verkhratsky_2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has also been shown, that tissue-injury related <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> signalling plays a crucial role in the phenotypic transformation of microglia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-berkicserep&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berkicserep-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ramified">Ramified</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ramified"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This form of microglial cell is commonly found at specific locations throughout the entire brain and spinal cord in the absence of foreign material or dying cells. This "resting" form of microglia is composed of long branching processes and a small cellular body. Unlike the amoeboid forms of microglia, the cell body of the ramified form remains in place while its branches are constantly moving and surveying the surrounding area. The branches are very sensitive to small changes in physiological condition and require very specific culture conditions to observe <a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Verkhratsky&#95;2013&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verkhratsky_2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike <a href="#Reactive_(activated)">activated</a> or <a href="#Amoeboid">ameboid</a> microglia, ramified microglia do not phagocytose cells and secrete fewer immunomolecules (including the <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_I" title="MHC class I">MHC class I</a>/<a href="/wiki/MHC_class_II" title="MHC class II">II</a> proteins). Microglia in this state are able to search for and identify immune threats while maintaining homeostasis in the CNS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Christensen&#95;2006&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen_2006-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Davis&#95;1994&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_1994-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although this is considered the resting state, microglia in this form are still extremely active in chemically surveying the environment. Ramified microglia can be transformed into the activated form at any time in response to injury or threat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Verkhratsky&#95;2013&#95;19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verkhratsky_2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Reactive_(activated)"><span id="Reactive_.28activated.29"></span>Reactive (activated)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Reactive (activated)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Although historically frequently used, the term "activated" microglia should be replaced by "reactive" microglia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Indeed, apparently quiescent microglia are not devoid of active functions and the "activation" term is misleading as it tends to indicate an "all or nothing" polarization of cell reactivity. The marker <a href="/wiki/Iba1" class="mw-redirect" title="Iba1">Iba1</a>, which is upregulated in reactive microglia, is often used to visualize these cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Non-phagocytic">Non-phagocytic</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Non-phagocytic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This state is actually part of a graded response as microglia move from their ramified form to their fully active phagocytic form. Microglia can be activated by a variety of factors including: pro-inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>, cell <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a> factors, lipopolysaccharide, and changes in extracellular potassium (indicative of ruptured cells). Once activated the cells undergo several key morphological changes including the thickening and retraction of branches, uptake of MHC class I/II proteins, expression of immunomolecules, secretion of <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> factors, secretion of recruitment molecules, and secretion of pro-inflammatory signaling molecules (resulting in a pro-inflammation signal cascade). Activated non-phagocytic microglia generally appear as "bushy", "rods", or small ameboids depending on how far along the ramified to full phagocytic transformation continuum they are. In addition, the microglia also undergo rapid proliferation in order to increase their numbers. From a strictly morphological perspective, the variation in microglial form along the continuum is associated with changing morphological complexity and can be quantitated using the methods of fractal analysis, which have proven sensitive to even subtle, visually undetectable changes associated with different morphologies in different pathological states.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Christensen&#95;2006&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen_2006-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jelinek&#95;2005&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jelinek_2005-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Phagocytic">Phagocytic</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Phagocytic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Activated phagocytic microglia are the maximally immune-responsive form of microglia. These cells generally take on a large, ameboid shape, although some variance has been observed. In addition to having the antigen presenting, <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> and inflammation-mediating signaling of activated non-phagocytic microglia, they are also able to phagocytose foreign materials and display the resulting immunomolecules for <a href="/wiki/T-cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cell">T-cell</a> activation. Phagocytic microglia travel to the site of the injury, engulf the offending material, and secrete pro-inflammatory factors to promote more cells to proliferate and do the same. Activated phagocytic microglia also interact with <a href="/wiki/Astrocyte" title="Astrocyte">astrocytes</a> and neural cells to fight off any infection or inflammation as quickly as possible with minimal damage to healthy brain cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Amoeboid">Amoeboid</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Amoeboid"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This shape allows the microglia free movement throughout the neural tissue, which allows it to fulfill its role as a scavenger cell. Amoeboid microglia are able to phagocytose debris, but do not fulfill the same antigen-presenting and inflammatory roles as <a href="#Reactive_(activated)">activated microglia</a>. Amoeboid microglia are especially prevalent during the development and rewiring of the brain, when there are large amounts of extracellular debris and <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptotic cells</a> to remove. This form of microglial cell is found mainly within the perinatal <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a> areas in the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_callosum" title="Corpus callosum">corpus callosum</a> known as the "Fountains of Microglia".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Christensen&#95;2006&#95;22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen_2006-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ferrer&#95;1990&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrer_1990-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Perivascular">Perivascular</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Perivascular"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Unlike the other types of microglia mentioned above, "perivascular" microglia refers to the location of the cell, rather than its form/function. Perivascular microglia are however often confused with perivascular macrophages (PVMs),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frontier&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frontier-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which are found encased within the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Basal_lamina" title="Basal lamina">basal lamina</a>, so care must be taken to determine which of these two cell types authors of publications are referring to. PVMs, unlike normal microglia, are replaced by <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>-derived precursor cells on a regular basis, and express <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_II" title="MHC class II">MHC class II</a> antigens regardless of their environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Juxtavascular">Juxtavascular</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Juxtavascular"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>"Perivascular microglia" and "juxtavascular microglia" are different names for the same type of cell. Confusion has arisen due to the misuse of the term perivascular microglia to refer to perivascular macrophages,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frontier&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frontier-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which are a different type of cell. Juxtavascular microglia/perivascular microglia are found making direct contact with the <a href="/wiki/Basal_lamina" title="Basal lamina">basal lamina</a> wall of blood vessels but are not found within the walls. In this position they can interact with both endothelial cells and <a href="/wiki/Pericyte" title="Pericyte">pericytes</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Like perivascular cells, they express <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_II" title="MHC class II">MHC class II</a> proteins even at low levels of inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> activity. Unlike perivascular cells, but similar to other microglia, juxtavascular microglia do not exhibit rapid turnover or replacement with <a href="/wiki/Myeloid_progenitor_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Myeloid progenitor cells">myeloid precursor cells</a> on a regular basis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Functions">Functions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Functions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Purinergic_signalling_Microglia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Purinergic_signalling_Microglia.jpg/250px-Purinergic_signalling_Microglia.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="184" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Purinergic_signalling_Microglia.jpg/500px-Purinergic_signalling_Microglia.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="843" data-file-height="621" /></a><figcaption>Activation of microglia via <a href="/wiki/Purinergic_signalling" title="Purinergic signalling">purinergic signalling</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Microglial cells fulfill a variety of different tasks within the CNS mainly related to both immune response and maintaining homeostasis. The following are some of the major known functions carried out by these cells.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Scavenging">Scavenging</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scavenging"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In addition to being very sensitive to small changes in their environment, each microglial cell also physically surveys its domain on a regular basis. This action is carried out in the ameboid and resting states via highly motile microglial processes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nimmerjahn&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmerjahn-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While moving through its set region, if the microglial cell finds any foreign material, damaged cells, <a href="/wiki/Apoptotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Apoptotic">apoptotic</a> cells, <a href="/wiki/Neurofibrillary_tangle" title="Neurofibrillary tangle">neurofibrillary tangles</a>, DNA fragments, or <a href="/wiki/Senile_plaque" class="mw-redirect" title="Senile plaque">plaques</a> it will activate and phagocytose the material or cell. In this manner microglial cells also act as "housekeepers", cleaning up random cellular debris.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During prenatal development of the brain, microglial cells play a role regulating numbers of neural precursor cells<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>, and removing apoptotic neurons. There is also evidence that microglia can refine synaptic circuitry by engulfing and eliminating synapses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Post development, the majority of dead or apoptotic cells are found in the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a> and the subcortical <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a>. This may explain why the majority of ameboid microglial cells are found within the "fountains of microglia" in the cerebral cortex.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ferrer&#95;1990&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrer_1990-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phagocytosis">Phagocytosis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Phagocytosis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The main role of microglia, <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>, involves the engulfing of various materials. Engulfed materials generally consist of cellular debris, <a href="/wiki/Lipids" class="mw-redirect" title="Lipids">lipids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apoptotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Apoptotic">apoptotic</a> cells in the non-inflamed state, and invading <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, or other foreign materials in the inflamed state. Once the microglial cell is "full" it stops <a href="/wiki/Phagocytic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phagocytic">phagocytic</a> activity and changes into a relatively non-reactive <a href="/wiki/Gitter_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Gitter cell">gitter cell</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Extracellular_signaling">Extracellular signaling</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Extracellular signaling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A large part of microglial cell's role in the brain is maintaining <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> in non-infected regions and promoting <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> in infected or damaged tissue. Microglia accomplish this through an extremely complicated series of extracellular signaling molecules which allow them to communicate with other microglia, <a href="/wiki/Astrocyte" title="Astrocyte">astrocytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, <a href="/wiki/T-cells" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cells">T-cells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Myeloid_progenitor_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Myeloid progenitor cell">myeloid progenitor cells</a>. As mentioned above the <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> IFN-γ can be used to activate microglial cells. In addition, after becoming activated with IFN-γ, microglia also release more IFN-γ into the extracellular space. This activates more microglia and starts a cytokine induced activation cascade rapidly activating all nearby microglia. Microglia-produced TNF-α causes neural tissue to undergo <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> and increases inflammation. <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_8" title="Interleukin 8">IL-8</a> promotes <a href="/wiki/B-cell" class="mw-redirect" title="B-cell">B-cell</a> growth and differentiation, allowing it to assist microglia in fighting infection. Another cytokine, <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_1" class="mw-redirect" title="Interleukin 1">IL-1</a>, inhibits the cytokines <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_10" title="Interleukin 10">IL-10</a> and <a href="/wiki/TGF-%CE%B2" class="mw-redirect" title="TGF-β">TGF-β</a>, which downregulate <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presentation" title="Antigen presentation">antigen presentation</a> and pro-inflammatory signaling. Additional <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Dendritic cells">dendritic cells</a> and T-cells are recruited to the site of injury through the microglial production of the <a href="/wiki/Chemotactic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemotactic">chemotactic</a> molecules like <a href="/wiki/CCL22" title="CCL22">MDC</a>, IL-8, and <a href="/wiki/CCL19" title="CCL19">MIP-3β</a>. Finally, PGE<sub>2</sub> and other <a href="/wiki/Prostanoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Prostanoids">prostanoids</a> prevent <a href="/wiki/Chronic_inflammation" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronic inflammation">chronic inflammation</a> by inhibiting microglial pro-inflammatory response and downregulating <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T helper cell">Th1</a> (T-helper cell) response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Antigen_presentation">Antigen presentation</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Antigen presentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As mentioned above, resident non-activated microglia act as poor <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presenting_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigen presenting cells">antigen presenting cells</a> due to their lack of MHC class I/II proteins. Upon activation they rapidly express MHC class I/II proteins and quickly become efficient antigen presenters. In some cases, microglia can also be activated by IFN-γ to present <a href="/wiki/Antigens" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigens">antigens</a>, but do not function as effectively as if they had undergone uptake of MHC class I/II proteins. During <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>, <a href="/wiki/T-cells" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cells">T-cells</a> cross the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a> thanks to specialized surface markers and then directly bind to microglia in order to receive <a href="/wiki/Antigens" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigens">antigens</a>. Once they have been presented with antigens, <a href="/wiki/T-cells" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cells">T-cells</a> go on to fulfill a variety of roles including pro-inflammatory recruitment, formation of immunomemories, secretion of <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> materials, and direct attacks on the plasma membranes of foreign cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cytotoxicity">Cytotoxicity</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cytotoxicity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In addition to being able to destroy infectious organisms through cell to cell contact via <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>, microglia can also release a variety of <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> substances.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia in culture secrete large amounts of hydrogen peroxide and nitric oxide in a process known as '<a href="/wiki/Respiratory_burst" title="Respiratory burst">respiratory burst</a>'. Both of these chemicals can directly damage cells and lead to neuronal cell death. <a href="/wiki/Proteases" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteases">Proteases</a> secreted by microglia <a href="/wiki/Catabolic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catabolic">catabolise</a> specific proteins causing direct cellular damage, while <a href="/wiki/Cytokines" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytokines">cytokines</a> like <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_1" class="mw-redirect" title="Interleukin 1">IL-1</a> promote <a href="/wiki/Demyelination" class="mw-redirect" title="Demyelination">demyelination</a> of neuronal axons. Finally, microglia can injure neurons through <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">NMDA receptor</a>-mediated processes by secreting <a href="/wiki/Glutamate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aspartate" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quinolinic_acid" title="Quinolinic acid">quinolinic acid</a>. Cytotoxic secretion is aimed at destroying infected neurons, virus, and bacteria, but can also cause large amounts of collateral neural damage. As a result, chronic inflammatory response can result in large scale neural damage as the microglia ravage the brain in an attempt to destroy the invading infection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edaravone" title="Edaravone">Edaravone</a>, a radical scavenger, precludes oxidative neurotoxicity precipitated by activated microglia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Synaptic_stripping">Synaptic stripping</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Synaptic stripping"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In a phenomenon first noticed in spinal lesions by Blinzinger and Kreutzberg in 1968, post-inflammation microglia remove the branches from nerves near damaged tissue. This helps promote regrowth and remapping of damaged <a href="/wiki/Neural_circuitry" class="mw-redirect" title="Neural circuitry">neural circuitry</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has also been shown that microglia are involved in the process of synaptic pruning during brain development.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Soteros&#95;2021&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soteros_2021-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Promotion_of_repair">Promotion of repair</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Promotion of repair"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Post-inflammation, microglia undergo several steps to promote regrowth of neural tissue. These include synaptic stripping, secretion of anti-inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokines" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytokines">cytokines</a>, recruitment of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astrocyte" title="Astrocyte">astrocytes</a> to the damaged area, and formation of <a href="/wiki/Gitter_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Gitter cell">gitter cells</a>. Without microglial cells regrowth and remapping would be considerably slower in the resident areas of the CNS and almost impossible in many of the vascular systems surrounding the brain and eyes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1995&#95;8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1995-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ritter&#95;2006&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritter_2006-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent research verified, that microglial processes constantly monitor neuronal functions through specialized somatic junctions, and sense the "well-being" of nerve cells. Via this intercellular communication pathway, microglia are capable of exerting robust neuroprotective effects, contributing significantly to repair after brain injury.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-epub.ub.uni-muenchen.de&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epub.ub.uni-muenchen.de-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microglia have also been shown to contribute to proper brain development, through contacting immature, developing neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Development">Development</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png/330px-Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png" decoding="async" width="268" height="129" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png/500px-Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png/960px-Microgliogenesis_at_a_glance.png 2x" data-file-width="4338" data-file-height="2089" /></a><figcaption>Origin and emergence of microglia in the CNS</figcaption></figure>
<p>For a long time it was thought that microglial cells differentiate in the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell" title="Hematopoietic stem cell">hematopoietic stem cells</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Progenitor_cell" title="Progenitor cell">progenitors</a> of all blood cells. However, recent studies show that microglia originate in the yolk sac during a remarkably restricted embryonal period and populate the brain parenchyma guided by a precisely orchestrated molecular process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dermitzakis&#95;2023&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dermitzakis_2023-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Yolk sac progenitor cells require activation <a href="/wiki/Colony_stimulating_factor_1_receptor" title="Colony stimulating factor 1 receptor">colony stimulating factor 1 receptor</a> (CSF1R) for migration into the brain and differentiation into microglia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, the greatest contribution to microglial repopulation is based upon its local self-renewal, both in steady state and disease, while circulating monocytes may also contribute to a lesser extent, especially in disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dermitzakis&#95;2023&#95;5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dermitzakis_2023-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Monocytes can also differentiate into <a href="/wiki/Myeloid_dendritic_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Myeloid dendritic cells">myeloid dendritic cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macrophages" class="mw-redirect" title="Macrophages">macrophages</a> in the peripheral systems. Like macrophages in the rest of the body, microglia use <a href="/wiki/Phagocytic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phagocytic">phagocytic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> mechanisms to destroy foreign materials. Microglia and macrophages both contribute to the immune response by acting as <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presenting_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigen presenting cells">antigen presenting cells</a>, as well as promoting inflammation and homeostatic mechanisms within the body by secreting <a href="/wiki/Cytokines" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytokines">cytokines</a> and other signaling molecules.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In their downregulated form, microglia lack the <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_I" title="MHC class I">MHC class I</a>/<a href="/wiki/MHC_class_II" title="MHC class II">MHC class II</a> proteins, IFN-γ <a href="/wiki/Cytokines" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytokines">cytokines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cd45" class="mw-redirect" title="Cd45">CD45 antigens</a>, and many other surface receptors required to act in the antigen-presenting, <a href="/wiki/Phagocytic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phagocytic">phagocytic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> roles that distinguish normal macrophages. Microglia also differ from macrophages in that they are much more tightly regulated spatially and temporally in order to maintain a precise immune response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aloisi&#95;2001&#95;21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aloisi_2001-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another difference between microglia and other cells that differentiate from <a href="/wiki/Myeloid_progenitor_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Myeloid progenitor cells">myeloid progenitor cells</a> is the turnover rate. Macrophages and <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Dendritic cells">dendritic cells</a> are constantly being used up and replaced by myeloid progenitor cells which differentiate into the needed type. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>, it would be fairly difficult for the body to constantly replace microglia. Therefore, instead of constantly being replaced with myeloid progenitor cells, the microglia maintain their status quo while in their quiescent state, and then, when they are activated, they rapidly proliferate in order to keep their numbers up. Bone chimera studies have shown, however, that in cases of extreme <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a> will weaken, and microglia will be replaced with haematogenous, marrow-derived cells, namely myeloid progenitor cells and macrophages. Once the infection has decreased the disconnect between peripheral and central systems is reestablished and only microglia are present for the recovery and regrowth period.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gehrmann&#95;1996&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrmann_1996-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Aging">Aging</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aging"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Microglia undergo a burst of <a href="/wiki/Mitotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitotic">mitotic</a> activity during injury; this proliferation is followed by <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> to reduce the cell numbers back to baseline.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Activation of microglia places a load on the <a href="/wiki/Anabolic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabolic">anabolic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catabolic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catabolic">catabolic</a> machinery of the cells causing activated microglia to die sooner than non-activated cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To compensate for microglial loss over time, microglia undergo mitosis and bone marrow derived <a href="/wiki/Progenitor_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Progenitor cells">progenitor cells</a> migrate into the brain via the <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a> and vasculature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Accumulation of minor neuronal damage that occurs during normal aging can transform microglia into enlarged and activated cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mrak&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mrak-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  These chronic, age-associated increases in microglial activation and IL-1 expression may contribute to increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> with advancing age through favoring neuritic plaque formation in susceptible patients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mrak&#95;44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mrak-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  DNA damage might contribute to age-associated microglial activation. Another factor might be the accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Advanced_glycation_end_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Advanced glycation end product">advanced glycation endproducts</a>, which accumulate with aging.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mrak&#95;44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mrak-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These proteins are strongly resistant to proteolytic processes and promote protein <a href="/wiki/Cross-link" title="Cross-link">cross-linking</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mrak&#95;44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mrak-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Research has discovered dystrophic (defective development) human microglia. "These cells are characterized by abnormalities in their cytoplasmic structure, such as deramified, atrophic, fragmented or unusually tortuous processes, frequently bearing spheroidal or bulbous swellings."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The incidence of dystrophic microglia increases with aging.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Microglial degeneration and death have been reported in research on <a href="/wiki/Transmissible_spongiform_encephalopathies" class="mw-redirect" title="Transmissible spongiform encephalopathies">prion disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a> and Alzheimer's disease, indicating that microglial deterioration might be involved in neurodegenerative diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  A complication of this theory is the fact that it is difficult to distinguish between "activated" and "dystrophic" microglia in the human brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Streita&#95;43-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Streita-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In mice, it has been shown that CD22 blockade restores homeostatic microglial phagocytosis in aging brains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-J.V.&#95;Pluvinage&#95;et&#95;al.&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J.V._Pluvinage_et_al.-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Microglial_cells.tif" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Microglial_cells.tif/lossy-page1-250px-Microglial_cells.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Microglial_cells.tif/lossy-page1-375px-Microglial_cells.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Microglial_cells.tif/lossy-page1-500px-Microglial_cells.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="2048" /></a><figcaption>image of microglia</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Clinical significance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Role_of_microglia_in_disease" title="Role of microglia in disease">Role of microglia in disease</a></div><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Step-by-step_guide_for_analyzing_microglia_phenotypes.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Step-by-step_guide_for_analyzing_microglia_phenotypes.png/250px-Step-by-step_guide_for_analyzing_microglia_phenotypes.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="336" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Step-by-step_guide_for_analyzing_microglia_phenotypes.png/500px-Step-by-step_guide_for_analyzing_microglia_phenotypes.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1978" data-file-height="2656" /></a><figcaption>Step-by-step guide for analyzing microglia phenotypes</figcaption></figure><p>Microglia are the primary immune cells of the central nervous system, similar to peripheral macrophages. They respond to pathogens and injury by changing morphology and migrating to the site of infection/injury, where they destroy pathogens and remove damaged cells. As part of their response they secrete cytokines, chemokines, prostaglandins, and reactive oxygen species, which help to direct the immune response. Additionally, they are instrumental in the resolution of the inflammatory response, through the production of anti-inflammatory cytokines. Microglia have also been extensively studied for their harmful roles in neurodegenerative diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson&#39;s disease">Parkinson's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">multiple sclerosis</a>, as well as cardiac diseases, <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma" title="Glaucoma">glaucoma</a>, and viral and bacterial infections. There is accumulating evidence that <a href="/wiki/Immune_dysregulation" title="Immune dysregulation">immune dysregulation</a> contributes to the pathophysiology of <a href="/wiki/Obsessive%E2%80%93compulsive_disorder" title="Obsessive–compulsive disorder">obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tourette_syndrome" title="Tourette syndrome">Tourette syndrome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/PANDAS" title="PANDAS">pediatric autoimmune neuropsychiatric disorders associated with streptococcal infections (PANDAS)</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since microglia rapidly react to even subtle alterations in central nervous system homeostasis, they can be seen as sensors for neurological dysfunctions or disorders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schwabenland&#95;2021&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwabenland_2021-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the event of brain pathologies, the microglial phenotype is certainly altered.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schwabenland&#95;2021&#95;47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwabenland_2021-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Therefore, analyzing microglia can be a sensitive tool to diagnose and characterize central nervous system disorders in any given tissue specimen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schwabenland&#95;2021&#95;47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwabenland_2021-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In particular, the microglial cell density, cell shape, distribution pattern, distinct microglial phenotypes and interactions with other cell types should be evaluated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schwabenland&#95;2021&#95;47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwabenland_2021-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Sensome_genetics">Sensome genetics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microglia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sensome genetics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The microglial sensome is a relatively new biological concept that appears to be playing a large role in <a href="/wiki/Neural_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Neural development">neurodevelopment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neurodegeneration" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurodegeneration">neurodegeneration</a>. The sensome refers to the unique grouping of protein <a href="/wiki/Primary_transcript" title="Primary transcript">transcripts</a> used for sensing <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbes</a>. In other words, the sensome represents the genes required for the proteins used to sense molecules within the body. The sensome can be analyzed with a variety of methods including <a href="/wiki/Real-time_polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Real-time polymerase chain reaction">qPCR</a>, <a href="/wiki/RNA-seq" class="mw-redirect" title="RNA-seq">RNA-seq</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">microarray analysis</a>, and direct RNA sequencing. Genes included in the sensome code for receptors and <a href="/wiki/Transmembrane_proteins" class="mw-redirect" title="Transmembrane proteins">transmembrane proteins</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">plasma membrane</a> that are more highly expressed in microglia compared to neurons. It does not include secreted proteins or transmembrane proteins specific to membrane bound organelles, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hickman&#95;2013&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hickman_2013-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The plurality of identified sensome genes code for pattern recognition receptors, however, there are a large variety of included genes. Microglial share a similar sensome to other macrophages, however they contain 22 unique genes, 16 of which are used for interaction with endogenous ligands. These differences create a unique microglial biomarker that includes over 40 genes including <a href="/wiki/P2Y12" title="P2Y12">P2ry12</a> and <a href="/wiki/HEXB" title="HEXB">HEXB</a>. DAP12 (<a href="/wiki/TYROBP" title="TYROBP">TYROBP</a>) appears to play an important role in sensome protein interaction, acting as a signalling adaptor and a regulatory protein.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hickman&#95;2013&#95;48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hickman_2013-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The regulation of genes within the sensome must be able to change in order to respond to potential harm. Microglia can take on the role of <a href="/wiki/Neuroprotection" title="Neuroprotection">neuroprotection</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxicity" title="Neurotoxicity">neurotoxicity</a> in order to face these dangers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For these reasons, it is suspected that the sensome may be playing a role in neurodegeneration. Sensome genes that are upregulated with aging are mostly involved in sensing infectious microbial ligands while those that are downregulated are mostly involved in sensing endogenous ligands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hickman&#95;2013&#95;48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hickman_2013-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This analysis suggests a glial-specific regulation favoring neuroprotection in natural neurodegeneration. This is in contrast to the shift towards neurotoxicity seen in neurodegenerative diseases.
</p><p>The sensome can also play a role in neurodevelopment. Early-life brain infection results in microglia that are hypersensitive to later immune stimuli. When exposed to infection, there is an upregulation of sensome genes involved in <a href="/wiki/Neuroinflammation" title="Neuroinflammation">neuroinflammation</a> and a downregulation of genes that are involved with neuroplasticity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26872419&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26872419-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The sensome's ability to alter neurodevelopment may however be able to combat disease. The deletion of <a href="/wiki/CX3CL1" title="CX3CL1">CX3CL1</a>, a highly expressed sensome gene, in rodent models of <a href="/wiki/Rett_syndrome" title="Rett syndrome">Rett syndrome</a> resulted in improved health and longer lifespan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26883520&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26883520-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The downregulation of Cx<sub>3</sub>cr1 in humans without Rett syndrome is associated with symptoms similar to <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26285829&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26285829-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This suggests that the sensome not only plays a role in various developmental disorders, but also requires tight regulation in order to maintain a disease-free state.
</p>

</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-Filiano&#95;2015-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Filiano_2015_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"
